title="Polynesians">Polynesian peoples</a></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>M&#257;ori</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/&#712;/: primary stress follows">&#712;</span><span title="\'m\' in \'my\'">m</span><span title="/a&#650;/: \'ou\' in \'mouth\'">a&#650;</span><span title="\'r\' in \'rye\'">r</span><span title="/i/: \'y\' in \'happy\'">i</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <small>M&#257;ori pronunciation:&#160;</small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/M%C4%81ori" title="Help:IPA/M&#257;ori">[&#712;ma&#720;&#596;&#638;i]</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rar-M%C4%81ori.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" decoding="async" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a></span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ec/Rar-M%C4%81ori.ogg" title="Rar-M&#257;ori.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>) are the <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_Oceania" title="Indigenous peoples of Oceania">indigenous</a> <a href="/wiki/Polynesians" title="Polynesians">Polynesian people</a> of <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>. M&#257;ori originated with settlers from eastern <a href="/wiki/Polynesia" title="Polynesia">Polynesia</a>, who arrived in New Zealand in several waves of canoe voyages some time between 1250 and 1300.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Over several centuries in isolation, the Polynesian settlers developed <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_culture" title="M&#257;ori culture">a unique culture</a>, with their own language, a rich mythology, and distinctive crafts and performing arts. Early M&#257;ori formed tribal groups based on eastern Polynesian social customs and organisation. Horticulture flourished using plants they introduced; later, a prominent <a href="/wiki/Warrior" title="Warrior">warrior</a> culture emerged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-4">[4]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The arrival of Europeans to New Zealand, starting in the 17th century, brought enormous changes to the M&#257;ori way of life. M&#257;ori people gradually adopted many aspects of Western society and culture. Initial relations between M&#257;ori and Europeans were largely amicable, and with the signing of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Waitangi" title="Treaty of Waitangi">Treaty of Waitangi</a> in 1840, the two cultures coexisted as part of a new British colony. Rising tensions over disputed land sales led to conflict in the 1860s. Social upheaval, decades of conflict and epidemics of introduced disease took a devastating toll on the M&#257;ori population, which fell dramatically. By the start of the 20th century, the M&#257;ori population had begun to recover, and efforts have been made to increase their standing in wider New Zealand society and achieve social justice. Traditional M&#257;ori culture has thereby enjoyed a significant revival, which was further bolstered by a <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_protest_movement" title="M&#257;ori protest movement">M&#257;ori protest movement</a> that emerged in the 1960s.\n</p><p>In the 2013 census, there were approximately 600,000 people in New Zealand identifying as M&#257;ori, making up roughly 15 percent of the national population. They are the second-largest ethnic group in New Zealand, after European New Zealanders ("<span lang="en" title="English language text"><a href="/wiki/P%C4%81keh%C4%81" title="P&#257;keh&#257;">P&#257;keh&#257;</a></span>"). In addition, more than 140,000 M&#257;ori live in Australia. The <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_language" title="M&#257;ori language">M&#257;ori language</a> is spoken to some extent by about a fifth of all M&#257;ori, representing 3 per cent of the total population. M&#257;ori are active in all spheres of New Zealand culture and society, with independent representation in areas such as media, politics and sport.\n</p><p>Disproportionate numbers of M&#257;ori face significant economic and social obstacles, and generally have lower life expectancies and incomes compared with other New Zealand ethnic groups. They suffer higher levels of crime, health problems, and educational under-achievement. A number of socioeconomic initiatives have been instigated with the aim of "closing the gap" between M&#257;ori and other New Zealanders. Political and economic redress for historical grievances is also ongoing (see <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Waitangi_claims_and_settlements" title="Treaty of Waitangi claims and settlements">Treaty of Waitangi claims and settlements</a>).\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" class="toclevel-2 tocsection-8"><a href="#Treaty_with_the_British_Crown_(1840)"><span class="tocnumber">3.5</span> <span class="toctext">Treaty with the British Crown (1840)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-9"><a href="#Land_disputes_and_conflict"><span class="tocnumber">3.6</span> <span class="toctext">Land disputes and conflict</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10"><a href="#Decline_and_revival"><span class="tocnumber">3.7</span> <span class="toctext">Decline and revival</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-11"><a href="#Recent_history_(1960s&#8211;present)"><span class="tocnumber">3.8</span> <span class="toctext">Recent history (1960s&#8211;present)</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-12"><a href="#Demographics"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Demographics</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-13"><a href="#Culture"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Culture</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Traditional_culture"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Traditional culture</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Belief_and_religion"><span class="tocnumber">5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Belief and religion</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Performing_arts"><span class="tocnumber">5.3</span> <span class="toctext">Performing arts</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Literature_and_media"><span class="tocnumber">5.4</span> <span class="toctext">Literature and media</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Sport"><span class="tocnumber">5.5</span> <span class="toctext">Sport</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#Language"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Language</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#Society"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Society</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#Historical_development"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">Historical development</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-22"><a href="#20th_century"><span class="tocnumber">7.1.1</span> <span class="toctext">20th century</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-23"><a href="#Marae,_hap&#363;_and_iwi"><span class="tocnumber">7.2</span> <span class="toctext">Marae, hap&#363; and iwi</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a href="#Socioeconomic_challenges"><span class="tocnumber">7.3</span> <span class="toctext">Socioeconomic challenges</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#Race_relations"><span class="tocnumber">7.4</span> <span class="toctext">Race relations</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#Commerce"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Commerce</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#Political_representation"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Political representation</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-31"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-32"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td class="mbox-image"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg/40px-Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg/60px-Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg/80px-Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></td>\n<td class="mbox-text plainlist">Look up <i><b><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/M%C4%81ori" title="wiktionary:M&#257;ori">M&#257;ori</a></b></i> in Wiktionary, the free dictionary.</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>In the <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_language" title="M&#257;ori language">M&#257;ori language</a>, the word <i lang="mi" title="M&#257;ori language text">m&#257;ori</i> means "normal", "natural" or "ordinary". In legends and oral traditions, the word distinguished ordinary mortal human beings&#8212;<i lang="mi" title="M&#257;ori language text">t&#257;ngata m&#257;ori</i>&#8212;from <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_religion" title="M&#257;ori religion">deities</a> and spirits (<i lang="mi" title="M&#257;ori language text">wairua</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="ref_Notei"><a href="#endnote_Notei">[i]</a></sup> Likewise, <i lang="mi" title="M&#257;ori language text">wai m&#257;ori</i> denotes "fresh water", as opposed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salt_water" title="Salt water">salt water</a>. There are <a href="/wiki/Cognate" title="Cognate">cognate</a> words in most <a href="/wiki/Polynesian_languages" title="Polynesian languages">Polynesian languages</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> all deriving from Proto-Polynesian <i>*ma(a)qoli</i>, which has the reconstructed meaning "true, real, genuine".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pollex_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-pollex-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eastern_Polynesian_Languages_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eastern_Polynesian_Languages-12">[12]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The spelling of "M&#257;ori" with or without the <a href="/wiki/Macron_(diacritic)" title="Macron (diacritic)">macron</a> is inconsistent in general-interest English-language media in New Zealand,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> although some newspapers and websites have adopted the standard M&#257;ori-language spelling (i.e., with macrons).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Naming_and_self-naming">Naming and self-naming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Naming and self-naming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Early visitors from Europe to New Zealand generally referred to the indigenous inhabitants as "New Zealanders" or as "natives".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> The M&#257;ori used the term <i lang="mi" title="M&#257;ori language text">M&#257;ori</i> to describe themselves in a pan-tribal sense.<sup class="reference" id="ref_Noteii"><a href="#endnote_Noteii">[ii]</a></sup> M&#257;ori people often use the term <i lang="mi" title="M&#257;ori language text"><a href="/wiki/Tangata_whenua" title="Tangata whenua">tangata whenua</a></i> (literally, "people of the land") to identify in a way that expresses their relationship with a particular area of land; a tribe may be the <i>tangata whenua</i> in one area, but not in another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The term can also refer to the M&#257;ori people as a whole in relation to New Zealand (<i lang="mi" title="M&#257;ori language text"><a href="/wiki/Aotearoa" title="Aotearoa">Aotearoa</a></i>) as a whole.\n</p><p>The M&#257;ori Purposes Act of 1947 required the use of the term "M&#257;ori" rather than "Native" in official usage. The Department of Native Affairs was renamed as the Department of M&#257;ori Affairs; since 1992 it has been known as <span lang="mi" style="font-style:normal;" title="M&#257;ori language text"><a href="/wiki/Te_Puni_K%C5%8Dkiri" title="Te Puni K&#333;kiri">Te Puni K&#333;kiri</a></span>, or the Ministry for M&#257;ori Development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Before 1974, the government required documented ancestry to determine the legal definition of "a M&#257;ori person". For example, bloodlines or percentage of M&#257;ori ancestry was used to determine whether a person should enroll on the general electoral roll or the separate <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_electorates" title="M&#257;ori electorates">M&#257;ori roll</a>. In 1947, the authorities determined that a man who was five-eighths M&#257;ori had improperly voted in the general parliamentary electorate of <a href="/wiki/Raglan_(New_Zealand_electorate)" title="Raglan (New Zealand electorate)">Raglan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The M&#257;ori Affairs Amendment Act 1974 changed the definition, allowing individuals to self-identify as to their cultural identity. In matters involving financial benefits provided by the government to people of M&#257;ori ethnicity&#8212;scholarships, for example, or <a href="/wiki/Waitangi_Tribunal" title="Waitangi Tribunal">Waitangi Tribunal</a> settlements&#8212;authorities generally require some documentation of ancestry or continuing cultural connection (such as acceptance by others as being of the people) but no minimum "blood" requirement exists as determined by the government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="ref_Noteiii"><a href="#endnote_Noteiii">[iii]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Polynesian_Migration.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="553" data-file-width="553" decoding="async" height="350" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Polynesian_Migration.svg/350px-Polynesian_Migration.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Polynesian_Migration.svg/525px-Polynesian_Migration.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Polynesian_Migration.svg/700px-Polynesian_Migration.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Polynesian_Migration.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The M&#257;ori settlement of New Zealand represents an end-point of a long chain of <a href="/wiki/Island_hopping" title="Island hopping">island-hopping</a> voyages in the <a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">South Pacific</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The most current reliable evidence strongly indicates that the initial settlement of New Zealand occurred around 1280 <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">CE</a>, at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Warm_Period" title="Medieval Warm Period">medieval warm period</a>. Previous dating of some <i lang="mi" title="M&#257;ori language text"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kiore" title="Kiore">kiore</a></i> (Polynesian rat) bones at 50&#8211;150 has now been shown to have been unreliable; new samples of bone (and now also of unequivocally rat-gnawed woody seed cases) match the 1280 date of the earliest archaeological sites and the beginning of sustained deforestation by humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lowe_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lowe-21">[21]</a></sup> M&#257;ori oral history describes the arrival of ancestors from <i><a href="/wiki/Hawaiki" title="Hawaiki">Hawaiki</a></i> (the mythical homeland in tropical Polynesia), in large ocean-going <i lang="mi" title="M&#257;ori language text"><a href="/wiki/Waka_(canoe)" title="Waka (canoe)">waka</a></i>. Migration accounts vary among tribes (<i lang="mi" title="M&#257;ori language text"><a href="/wiki/Iwi" title="Iwi">iwi</a></i>), whose members may identify with several waka in their genealogies (<i lang="mi" title="M&#257;ori language text"><a href="/wiki/Whakapapa" title="Whakapapa">whakapapa</a></i>). In the last few decades, <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial-DNA</a> (mtDNA) research has allowed an estimate to be made of the number of women in the founding population&#8212;between 50 and 100.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Evidence from archaeology, linguistics, and physical anthropology indicates that the first settlers came from east Polynesia and became the M&#257;ori. <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_linguistics" title="Evolutionary linguistics">Language-evolution</a> studies<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stuff.co.nz_807540_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stuff.co.nz_807540-24">[24]</a></sup> and mtDNA evidence<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> suggest that most Pacific populations originated from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taiwanese_aborigines" title="Taiwanese aborigines">Taiwanese aborigines</a> around 5,200 years ago (suggesting prior migration from the Asian or Chinese mainland).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> These ancestors moved down through <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NZ_Herald_144542_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-NZ_Herald_144542-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Analysis by Kayser <i>et al.</i> (2008) discovered that only 21% of the Maori-Polynesian autosomal gene pool is of Melanesian origin, with the rest (79%) being of East Asian origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Another study by Friedlaender <i>et al.</i> (2008) also confirmed that Polynesians are closer genetically to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Micronesian_people" title="Micronesian people">Micronesians</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taiwanese_Aborigines" title="Taiwanese Aborigines">Taiwanese Aborigines</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East_Asians" title="East Asians">East Asians</a>, than to <a href="/wiki/Melanesians" title="Melanesians">Melanesians</a>. The study concluded that Polynesians moved through Melanesia fairly rapidly, allowing only limited admixture between Austronesians and Melanesians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>  Thus the high frequencies of mtDNA B4a1a1 in the Polynesians are the result of drift and represent the descendants of a few East Asian females who mixed with Papuan males.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> The Polynesian population experienced a <a href="/wiki/Founder_effect" title="Founder effect">founder effect</a> and genetic drift.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Recent DNA studies show that the ancestors of the Polynesian people (including Maori) are from <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a> and from the northern <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>. They did not mix with the Melanesians natives but displaced them mostly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Archaic_period_.281280.E2.80.931500.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Archaic_period_(1280&#8211;1500)">Archaic period (1280&#8211;1500)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Archaic period (1280&#8211;1500)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The earliest period of M&#257;ori settlement is known as the "Archaic", "Moahunter" or "Colonisation" period. The eastern Polynesian ancestors of the M&#257;ori arrived in a forested land with <a href="/wiki/Birds_of_New_Zealand" title="Birds of New Zealand">abundant birdlife</a>, including several now extinct <i lang="mi" title="M&#257;ori language text"><a href="/wiki/Moa" title="Moa">moa</a></i> species weighing between 20 kilograms (44&#160;lb) and 250&#160;kg (550&#160;lb) each. Other species, also now extinct, included a swan, a goose and the giant <a href="/wiki/Haast%27s_eagle" title="Haast\'s eagle">Haast\'s eagle</a>, which preyed upon the <i>moa</i>. Marine mammals&#8212;seals in particular&#8212;thronged the coasts, with evidence of coastal colonies much further north than those which remain today<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Huge numbers of <i>moa</i> bones&#8212;estimated to be from between 29,000 and 90,000 birds&#8212;have been located at the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Waitaki_River" title="Waitaki River">Waitaki River</a>, between <a href="/wiki/Timaru" title="Timaru">Timaru</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oamaru" title="Oamaru">Oamaru</a> on the east coast of the <a href="/wiki/South_Island" title="South Island">South Island</a>. Further south, at the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Shag_River" title="Shag River">Shag River</a> (<i lang="mi" title="M&#257;ori language text">Waihemo</i>), evidence suggests that at least 6,000 <i>moa</i> were slaughtered by humans over a relatively short period of time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Archaeology has shown that the <a href="/wiki/Otago" title="Otago">Otago</a> region was the node of M&#257;ori cultural development during this time, and the majority of archaic settlements were on or within 10&#160;km (6&#160;mi) of the coast. It was common for people to establish small temporary camps far inland for seasonal hunting. Settlements ranged in size from 40 people (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Palliser_Bay" title="Palliser Bay">Palliser Bay</a> in Wellington) to between 300 and 400 people, with 40 buildings (such as at the Shag River).\n</p><p>The best-known and most extensively studied Archaic site is at <a href="/wiki/Wairau_Bar" title="Wairau Bar">Wairau Bar</a> in the South Island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The site is similar to eastern Polynesian <a href="/wiki/Nucleated_village" title="Nucleated village">nucleated villages</a>. <a href="/wiki/Radiocarbon_dating" title="Radiocarbon dating">Radiocarbon dating</a> shows the site was occupied from about 1288 to 1300. Due to tectonic forces, some of the Wairau Bar site is now underwater. Work on the Wairau Bar skeletons in 2010 showed that life expectancy was very short, the oldest skeleton being 39 and most people dying in their 20s. Most of the adults showed signs of dietary or infection stress. <a href="/wiki/Anemia" title="Anemia">Anemia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arthritis" title="Arthritis">arthritis</a> were common. Infections such as <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a> (TB) may have been present, as the symptoms were present in several skeletons. On average, the adults were taller than other South Pacific people, at 175 centimetres (5&#160;ft 9&#160;in) for males and 161&#160;cm (5&#160;ft 3&#160;in) for females.\n</p><p>The Archaic period is remarkable for the lack of weapons and fortifications so typical of the later "Classic" M&#257;ori,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moa66_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-moa66-37">[37]</a></sup> and for its distinctive "reel necklaces".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NgaKakano_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-NgaKakano-38">[38]</a></sup> From this period onward, some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_extinct_New_Zealand_animals#Extinctions_since_14th_century_M&#257;ori_settlement" title="List of extinct New Zealand animals">32 species of birds became extinct</a>, either through over-predation by humans and the <i lang="mi" title="M&#257;ori language text">kiore</i> and <i lang="mi" title="M&#257;ori language text"><a href="/wiki/Kur%C4%AB" title="Kur&#299;">kur&#299;</a></i> (dog) they introduced;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> repeated burning of the grassland that changed their habitat, or climate cooling, which appears to have occurred from about 1400&#8211;1450. The early M&#257;ori enjoyed a rich, varied diet of birds, fish, seals and shellfish. <i lang="mi" title="M&#257;ori language text">Moa</i> were also an important source of meat. According to Professor Allan Cooper, the people slaughtered to extinction most of the various lost species within 100 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Houghton,_Philip_1980_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Houghton,_Philip_1980-40">[40]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Work by <a href="/wiki/Helen_Leach" title="Helen Leach">Helen Leach</a> shows that M&#257;ori were using about 36 different food plants, although many required detoxification and long periods (12&#8211;24 hours) of cooking. D. Sutton\'s research on early M&#257;ori fertility found that first pregnancy occurred at about 20 years and the mean number of births was low, compared with other neolithic societies. The low number of births may have been due to the very low average life expectancy of 31&#8211;32 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Houghton,_Philip_1980_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Houghton,_Philip_1980-40">[40]</a></sup> Analysis of skeletons at Wairau Bar showed signs of a hard life, with many having had broken bones that had healed. This suggests that the people ate a balanced diet and enjoyed a supportive community that had the resources to support severely injured family members.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Classic_period_.281500.E2.80.931642.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Classic_period_(1500&#8211;1642)">Classic period (1500&#8211;1642)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Classic period (1500&#8211;1642)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Model_Of_Maori_Pa_On_Headland.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1599" decoding="async" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Model_Of_Maori_Pa_On_Headland.jpg/250px-Model_Of_Maori_Pa_On_Headland.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Model_Of_Maori_Pa_On_Headland.jpg/375px-Model_Of_Maori_Pa_On_Headland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Model_Of_Maori_Pa_On_Headland.jpg/500px-Model_Of_Maori_Pa_On_Headland.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Model_Of_Maori_Pa_On_Headland.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Model of a <i lang="mi" title="M&#257;ori language text"><a href="/wiki/P%C4%81" title="P&#257;">p&#257;</a></i> (hillfort) built on a headland. <i lang="mi" title="M&#257;ori language text">P&#257;</i> proliferated as competition and warfare increased among a growing population.</div></div></div>\n<p>The cooling of the climate, confirmed by a detailed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tree_ring" title="Tree ring">tree-ring</a> study near <a href="/wiki/Hokitika" title="Hokitika">Hokitika</a>, shows a significant, sudden and long-lasting <a href="/wiki/Little_Ice_Age" title="Little Ice Age">cooler period</a> from 1500. This coincided with a series of massive earthquakes in the South Island Alpine fault, a major earthquake in 1460 in the <a href="/wiki/Wellington" title="Wellington">Wellington</a> area,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tsunamis" title="Tsunamis">tsunamis</a> that destroyed many coastal settlements, and the extinction of the <i lang="mi" title="M&#257;ori language text">moa</i> and other food species. These were likely factors that led to sweeping changes in the M&#257;ori culture, which developed into the "Classic" period<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> that was in place at the time of European contact.\n</p><p>This period is characterised by finely made <i lang="mi" title="M&#257;ori language text"><a href="/wiki/Pounamu" title="Pounamu">pounamu</a></i> (greenstone) weapons and ornaments, elaborately carved canoes&#8212;a tradition that was later extended to and continued in elaborately carved meeting houses called <i lang="mi" title="M&#257;ori language text"><a href="/wiki/Wharenui" title="Wharenui">wharenui</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neich_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-neich-43">[43]</a></sup>&#8212;and a fierce <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warrior_culture" title="Warrior culture">warrior culture</a>. They developed <a href="/wiki/Hillfort" title="Hillfort">hillforts</a> known as <i lang="mi" title="M&#257;ori language text"><a href="/wiki/P%C4%81" title="P&#257;">p&#257;</a></i>, practiced <a href="/wiki/Cannibalism" title="Cannibalism">cannibalism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NZ_Herald_10462390_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-NZ_Herald_10462390-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> and built some of the largest <a href="/wiki/Waka_(canoe)" title="Waka (canoe)">war canoes</a> ever.\n</p><p>Around the year 1500, a group of M&#257;ori migrated east to the <a href="/wiki/Chatham_Islands" title="Chatham Islands">Chatham Islands</a>, where, by adapting to the local climate and the availability of resources, they developed into a people known as the <a href="/wiki/Moriori" title="Moriori">Moriori</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> related to but distinct from the M&#257;ori of mainland New Zealand. A notable feature of Moriori culture was an emphasis on <a href="/wiki/Pacifism" title="Pacifism">pacifism</a>. When a party of invading North Taranaki M&#257;ori arrived in 1835, few of the estimated Moriori population of 2,000 survived; they were killed outright and many were enslaved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The largest battle ever fought in New Zealand, the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hingakaka" title="Battle of Hingakaka">Battle of Hingakaka</a>, occurred around 1780&#8211;90, south of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=%C5%8Chaup%C5%8D&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="&#332;haup&#333; (page does not exist)">&#332;haup&#333;</a> on a ridge near <a href="/wiki/Lake_Ngaroto" title="Lake Ngaroto">Lake Ngaroto</a>. The battle was fought between about 7,000 warriors from a <a href="/wiki/Taranaki" title="Taranaki">Taranaki</a>-led force and a much smaller <a href="/wiki/Waikato" title="Waikato">Waikato</a> force under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Te_Rauangaanga" title="Te Rauangaanga">Te Rauangaanga</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Early_European_contact_.281642.E2.80.931840.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_European_contact_(1642&#8211;1840)">Early European contact (1642&#8211;1840)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Early European contact (1642&#8211;1840)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gilsemans_1642.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1948" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Gilsemans_1642.jpg/260px-Gilsemans_1642.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Gilsemans_1642.jpg/390px-Gilsemans_1642.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Gilsemans_1642.jpg/520px-Gilsemans_1642.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gilsemans_1642.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The first European impression of M&#257;ori, at <a href="/wiki/Golden_Bay" title="Golden Bay">Murderers\' Bay</a> in <a href="/wiki/Abel_Tasman" title="Abel Tasman">Abel Tasman</a>\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Travel_journal" title="Travel journal">travel journal</a> (1642).</div></div></div>\n<p>European settlement of New Zealand occurred in relatively recent historical times. New Zealand historian <a href="/wiki/Michael_King" title="Michael King">Michael King</a> in <i>The Penguin History Of New Zealand</i> describes the M&#257;ori as "the last major human community on earth untouched and unaffected by the wider world." Early European explorers, including <a href="/wiki/Abel_Tasman" title="Abel Tasman">Abel Tasman</a> (who arrived in 1642) and Captain <a href="/wiki/James_Cook" title="James Cook">James Cook</a> (who first visited in 1769), recorded their impressions of M&#257;ori. Initial contact between M&#257;ori and Europeans proved problematic and sometimes fatal, with several accounts of Europeans being cannibalised.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Louis_John_Steel_-_The_blowing_up_of_the_Boyd_Steele_(1889).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="418" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Louis_John_Steel_-_The_blowing_up_of_the_Boyd_Steele_%281889%29.jpg/260px-Louis_John_Steel_-_The_blowing_up_of_the_Boyd_Steele_%281889%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Louis_John_Steel_-_The_blowing_up_of_the_Boyd_Steele_%281889%29.jpg/390px-Louis_John_Steel_-_The_blowing_up_of_the_Boyd_Steele_%281889%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Louis_John_Steel_-_The_blowing_up_of_the_Boyd_Steele_%281889%29.jpg/520px-Louis_John_Steel_-_The_blowing_up_of_the_Boyd_Steele_%281889%29.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Louis_John_Steel_-_The_blowing_up_of_the_Boyd_Steele_(1889).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Blowing Up of the Boyd</i> by <a href="/wiki/Louis_John_Steele" title="Louis John Steele">Louis John Steele</a>, 1889</div></div></div>\n<p>From the 1780s, M&#257;ori encountered European and American <a href="/wiki/Seal_hunting" title="Seal hunting">sealers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Whaling" title="Whaling">whalers</a>; some M&#257;ori crewed on the foreign ships, with many crewing on whaling and sealing ships that operated in New Zealand waters. Some of the South Island crews were almost totally M&#257;ori. Between 1800 and 1820, there were 65 sealing voyages and 106 whaling voyages to New Zealand, mainly from Britain and Australia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> A trickle of escaped <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Convictism_in_Australia" title="Convictism in Australia">convicts from Australia</a> and deserters from visiting ships, as well as early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mission_(Christian)" title="Mission (Christian)">Christian missionaries</a>, also exposed the indigenous population to outside influences. In the <a href="/wiki/Boyd_massacre" title="Boyd massacre">Boyd Massacre</a> in 1809, M&#257;ori took hostage and killed 66 members of the crew and passengers of the sailing ship Boyd in apparent revenge for the captain whipping the son of a M&#257;ori chief. Given accounts of cannibalism in this attack, shipping companies and missionaries kept their distance, significantly reducing their contact with the M&#257;ori for several years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The runaways were of various standing within M&#257;ori society, ranging from slaves to high-ranking advisors. Some runaways remained little more than prisoners, while others abandoned European culture and identified as M&#257;ori. These Europeans "gone native" became known as <a href="/wiki/P%C4%81keh%C4%81_M%C4%81ori" title="P&#257;keh&#257; M&#257;ori">P&#257;keh&#257; M&#257;ori</a>. Many M&#257;ori valued them as a means to acquire European knowledge and technology, particularly firearms. When Whiria (<a href="/wiki/P%C5%8Dmare_II_(Ng%C4%81puhi)" title="P&#333;mare II (Ng&#257;puhi)">P&#333;mare II</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pom2_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pom2-52">[52]</a></sup> led a war-party against <a href="/wiki/T%C4%ABtore" title="T&#299;tore">T&#299;tore</a> in 1838, he had 131 Europeans among his warriors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Edward_Maning" title="Frederick Edward Maning">Frederick Edward Maning</a>, an early settler, wrote two lively accounts of life in these times, which have become classics of <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_literature" title="New Zealand literature">New Zealand literature</a>: <i>Old New Zealand</i> and <i>History of the War in the North of New Zealand against the Chief Heke</i>. European settlement of New Zealand increased steadily. By 1839, estimates placed the number of Europeans living among the M&#257;ori as high as 2,000; two-thirds lived in the North Island, especially in the <a href="/wiki/Northland_Peninsula" title="Northland Peninsula">Northland Peninsula</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Contact with Europeans led to a sharing of concepts. The M&#257;ori language was first written down by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Kendall" title="Thomas Kendall">Thomas Kendall</a> in 1815, in <i><a href="/wiki/A_korao_no_New_Zealand" title="A korao no New Zealand">A korao no New Zealand</a></i>; this was followed five years later by <i>A Grammar and Vocabulary of the New Zealand Language</i>, compiled by Professor <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Lee_(linguist)" title="Samuel Lee (linguist)">Samuel Lee</a> and aided by Kendall, Waikato M&#257;ori and the chief <a href="/wiki/Hongi_Hika" title="Hongi Hika">Hongi Hika</a>, on a visit to England in 1820. M&#257;ori quickly adopted writing as a means of sharing ideas, and many of their oral stories and poems were converted to the written form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>  Between February 1835 and January 1840, <a href="/wiki/William_Colenso" title="William Colenso">William Colenso</a> printed 74,000 M&#257;ori-language booklets from his press at <a href="/wiki/Paihia" title="Paihia">Paihia</a>. In 1843, the government distributed free gazettes to M&#257;ori called <i>Ko Te Karere O Nui Tireni.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> These contained information about law and crimes, with explanations and remarks about European customs, and were "designed to pass on official information to M&#257;ori and to encourage the idea that P&#257;keh&#257; and M&#257;ori were contracted together under the Treaty of Waitangi".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Between 1805 and 1840, the acquisition of <a href="/wiki/Musket" title="Musket">muskets</a> by tribes in close contact with European visitors upset the balance of power among M&#257;ori tribes. This led to a period of bloody <a href="/wiki/Endemic_warfare" title="Endemic warfare">intertribal warfare</a> known as the <a href="/wiki/Musket_Wars" title="Musket Wars">Musket Wars</a>, which resulted in the decimation of several tribes and the driving of others from their traditional territory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> It has been estimated that during this period the M&#257;ori population dropped from about 100,000 (in 1800) to between 50,000 and 80,000 by the wars\' end in 1843. The 1850s were a decade of relative stability and economic growth for M&#257;ori.\n</p><p>The picture is confused by uncertainty over how or if P&#257;keh&#257; M&#257;ori were counted, and the severe dislocation of many of the less powerful <i lang="mi" title="M&#257;ori language text">iwi</i> and <i lang="mi" title="M&#257;ori language text"><a href="/wiki/Hap%C5%AB" title="Hap&#363;">hap&#363;</a></i> (subtribes) during the wars. The smashing of normal society by the four decades of wars and the driving of peaceful tribes from their productive turangawaewae, such as the Moriori in the <a href="/wiki/Chatham_Islands" title="Chatham Islands">Chatham Islands</a> by invading forces from North Taranaki, had a catastrophic effect on these conquered tribes.\n</p><p>At the same time, the M&#257;ori suffered high mortality rates for new Eurasian infectious diseases, such as <a href="/wiki/Influenza" title="Influenza">influenza</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a> and <a href="/wiki/Measles" title="Measles">measles</a>, which killed an unknown number of M&#257;ori: estimates vary between ten and fifty percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The spread of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epidemics" title="Epidemics">epidemics</a> resulted largely from the M&#257;ori lacking acquired <a href="/wiki/Immunity_(medical)" title="Immunity (medical)">immunity</a> to the new diseases. A huge influx of European settlers in the 1870s increased contact among many of the indigenous people with the newcomers.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Te_Rangi_H%C4%ABroa" title="Te Rangi H&#299;roa">Te Rangi H&#299;roa</a> documents an epidemic caused by a respiratory disease that M&#257;ori called <i lang="mi" title="M&#257;ori language text">rewharewha</i>. It "decimated" populations in the early 19th century and "spread with extraordinary virulence throughout the North Island and even to the South... Measles, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Typhoid" title="Typhoid">typhoid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scarlet_fever" title="Scarlet fever">scarlet fever</a>, <a href="/wiki/Whooping_cough" title="Whooping cough">whooping cough</a> and almost everything, except <a href="/wiki/Plague_(disease)" title="Plague (disease)">plague</a> and <a href="/wiki/African_trypanosomiasis" title="African trypanosomiasis">sleeping sickness</a>, have taken their toll of M&#257;ori dead."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Economic changes also took a toll: migration of M&#257;ori workers into unhealthy swamplands to produce and export <a href="/wiki/Phormium" title="Phormium">flax</a> led to further mortality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Treaty_with_the_British_Crown_.281840.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Treaty_with_the_British_Crown_(1840)">Treaty with the British Crown (1840)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Treaty with the British Crown (1840)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;max-width:292px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:136px;max-width:136px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:218px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HoneHeke1845.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="581" data-file-width="356" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/HoneHeke1845.jpg/134px-HoneHeke1845.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/HoneHeke1845.jpg/201px-HoneHeke1845.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/HoneHeke1845.jpg/268px-HoneHeke1845.jpg 2x" width="134"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">One of the signatories of the treaty, <a href="/wiki/H%C5%8Dne_Heke" title="H&#333;ne Heke">H&#333;ne Heke</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ng%C4%81puhi" title="Ng&#257;puhi">Ng&#257;puhi</a> iwi, with his wife Hariata</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:218px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TamatiWakaNene1870s.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1319" data-file-width="903" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/TamatiWakaNene1870s.jpg/150px-TamatiWakaNene1870s.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/TamatiWakaNene1870s.jpg/225px-TamatiWakaNene1870s.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/TamatiWakaNene1870s.jpg/300px-TamatiWakaNene1870s.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/T%C4%81mati_W%C4%81ka_Nene" title="T&#257;mati W&#257;ka Nene">T&#257;mati W&#257;ka Nene</a> of Ng&#257;puhi was a signatory of the treaty, and was influential in convincing others to sign.</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<p>With increasing Christian <a href="/wiki/Missionary" title="Missionary">missionary</a> activity and growing European settlement in the 1830s, and with growing lawlessness in New Zealand, the <a href="/wiki/The_Crown" title="The Crown">British Crown</a> acceded to repeated requests from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Missionaries" title="Missionaries">missionaries</a> and some chiefs (<i><a href="/wiki/Rangatira" title="Rangatira">rangatira</a></i>) to intervene. Some freewheeling escaped convicts and seamen, as well as gunrunners and Americans actively worked against the British government by spreading rumours amongst the M&#257;ori that the government would oppress and mistreat them. Tamati Waka Nene, a pro-government chief, was angry that the government had not taken active steps to stop gunrunners selling weapons to rebels in Hokianga. In addition, France appeared to be showing interest in acquiring New Zealand to add to its stake in Polynesia. British immigrants believed that the French Catholic missionaries were spreading anti-British feeling. All of the chiefs who spoke against the Treaty on 5 February 1840 were Catholic. Years after the treaty was signed, Bishop <a href="/wiki/Jean_Baptiste_Pompallier" title="Jean Baptiste Pompallier">Pompallier</a> admitted that all the Catholic chiefs and especially Rewa, had consulted him for advice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ultimately, the British government sent Royal Navy Captain <a href="/wiki/William_Hobson" title="William Hobson">William Hobson</a> with instructions to negotiate a treaty between the British Crown and the people of New Zealand. Soon after arrival in New Zealand in February 1840, Hobson negotiated a treaty with North Island chiefs, later to become known as the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Waitangi" title="Treaty of Waitangi">Treaty of Waitangi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> In the end, 500 tribal chiefs and a small number of Europeans signed the Treaty, while some chiefs &#8212; such as <a href="/wiki/P%C5%8Dtatau_Te_Wherowhero" title="P&#333;tatau Te Wherowhero">Te Wherowhero</a> in Waikato<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> &#8212; refused to sign. The Treaty gave M&#257;ori the rights of <a href="/wiki/British_nationality_law" title="British nationality law">British subjects</a> and guaranteed M&#257;ori property rights and tribal autonomy, in return for accepting British <a href="/wiki/Sovereignty" title="Sovereignty">sovereignty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Considerable dispute continues over aspects of the Treaty of Waitangi. The original treaty was written mainly by Busby and translated into M&#257;ori by Henry Williams, who was moderately proficient in M&#257;ori, and his son William, who was more skilled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> They were handicapped by their imperfect M&#257;ori and the lack of exactly similar words in M&#257;ori, as well as by deep differences among the peoples on concepts of property rights and sovereignty, for example.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> At Waitangi the chiefs signed the M&#257;ori translation.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Land_disputes_and_conflict">Land disputes and conflict</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Land disputes and conflict">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_Wars" title="New Zealand Wars">New Zealand Wars</a></div>\n<p>Despite conflicting interpretations of the provisions of the Treaty of Waitangi, relations between M&#257;ori and Europeans during the early colonial period were largely peaceful. Many M&#257;ori groups set up substantial businesses, supplying food and other products for domestic and overseas markets. Among the early European settlers who learnt the M&#257;ori language and recorded <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_mythology" title="M&#257;ori mythology">M&#257;ori mythology</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_Edward_Grey" title="George Edward Grey">George Grey</a>, Governor of New Zealand from 1845&#8211;1855 and 1861&#8211;1868, stands out.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rewi_Manga_Maniapoto,_by_Gottfried_Lindauer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="1629" decoding="async" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Rewi_Manga_Maniapoto%2C_by_Gottfried_Lindauer.jpg/170px-Rewi_Manga_Maniapoto%2C_by_Gottfried_Lindauer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Rewi_Manga_Maniapoto%2C_by_Gottfried_Lindauer.jpg/255px-Rewi_Manga_Maniapoto%2C_by_Gottfried_Lindauer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Rewi_Manga_Maniapoto%2C_by_Gottfried_Lindauer.jpg/340px-Rewi_Manga_Maniapoto%2C_by_Gottfried_Lindauer.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rewi_Manga_Maniapoto,_by_Gottfried_Lindauer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A portrait of M&#257;ori man, by <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Lindauer" title="Gottfried Lindauer">Gottfried Lindauer</a>, 1882</div></div></div>\n<p>However, rising tensions over disputed land purchases and attempts by M&#257;ori in the <a href="/wiki/Waikato" title="Waikato">Waikato</a> to establish what some saw as a rival to the British system of royalty led to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Zealand_wars" title="New Zealand wars">New Zealand wars</a> in the 1860s. These conflicts started when rebel M&#257;ori attacked isolated settlers in Taranaki but were fought mainly between Crown troops&#8212;from both Britain and new regiments raised in Australia, aided by settlers and some allied M&#257;ori (known as <i>kupapa</i>)&#8212;and numerous M&#257;ori groups opposed to the disputed land sales, including some Waikato M&#257;ori.\n</p><p>While these conflicts resulted in few M&#257;ori (compared to the earlier Musket wars) or European deaths, the colonial government <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_land_confiscations" title="New Zealand land confiscations">confiscated</a> tracts of tribal land as punishment for what were called rebellions. In some cases the government confiscated land from tribes that had taken no part in the war, although this was almost immediately returned. Some of the confiscated land was returned to both kupapa and "rebel" M&#257;ori. Several minor conflicts also arose after the wars, including the incident at <a href="/wiki/Parihaka" title="Parihaka">Parihaka</a> in 1881 and the <a href="/wiki/Dog_Tax_War" title="Dog Tax War">Dog Tax War</a> from 1897&#8211;98.\n</p><p>The Native Land Acts of 1862 and 1865 established the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Native_Land_Court" title="Native Land Court">Native Land Court</a>, which was intended to transfer M&#257;ori land from communal ownership into individual household title as a means to assimilation and to facilitate greater sales to European immigrants. M&#257;ori land under individual title became available to be sold to the colonial government or to settlers in private sales. Between 1840 and 1890, M&#257;ori sold 95 percent of their land (63,000,000 of 66,000,000 acres (270,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup>) in 1890). In total 4 per cent of this was confiscated land, although about a quarter of this was returned. 300,000 acres was returned to Kupapa M&#257;ori mainly in the lower Waikato River Basin area. Individual M&#257;ori titleholders received considerable capital from these land sales, with some lower Waikato Chiefs being given 1000 pounds each. Disputes later arose over whether or not promised compensation in some sales was fully delivered. Some claim that later, the selling off of M&#257;ori land and the lack of appropriate skills hampered M&#257;ori participation in developing the New Zealand economy, eventually diminishing the capacity of many M&#257;ori to sustain themselves.\n</p><p>The M&#257;ori MP <a href="/wiki/Henare_Kaihau" title="Henare Kaihau">Henare Kaihau</a>, from Waiuku, who was executive head of the King Movement, worked alongside King <a href="/wiki/Mahuta_T%C4%81whiao" title="Mahuta T&#257;whiao">Mahuta</a> to sell land to the government. At that time the king sold 185,000 acres per year. In 1910 the M&#257;ori Land Conference at Waihi discussed selling a further 600,000 acres. King Mahuta had been successful in getting restitution for some blocks of land previously confiscated, and these were returned to the King in his name. Henare Kaihau invested all the money- 50,000 pounds- in an Auckland land company which collapsed; all 50,000 pounds of the kingitanga money was lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TawhiaoNLA.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="486" data-file-width="407" decoding="async" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/TawhiaoNLA.jpg/170px-TawhiaoNLA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/TawhiaoNLA.jpg/255px-TawhiaoNLA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/TawhiaoNLA.jpg/340px-TawhiaoNLA.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TawhiaoNLA.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/T%C4%81whiao" title="T&#257;whiao">T&#257;whiao</a>, the second M&#257;ori King</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1884 King <a href="/wiki/T%C4%81whiao" title="T&#257;whiao">T&#257;whiao</a> withdrew money from the kingitanga bank, Te Peeke o Aotearoa<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> to travel to London to see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Victoria_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Victoria of the United Kingdom">Queen Victoria</a> to try and persuade her to honour the Treaty between their peoples. He did not get past the <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_the_Colonies" title="Secretary of State for the Colonies">Secretary of State for the Colonies</a>, who said it was a New Zealand problem. Returning to New Zealand, the Premier <a href="/wiki/Robert_Stout" title="Robert Stout">Robert Stout</a> insisted that all events happening before 1863 were the responsibility of the Imperial Government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>\n</p><p>By 1891 M&#257;ori comprised just 10 per cent of the population but still owned 17 per cent of the land, although much of it was of poor quality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decline_and_revival">Decline and revival</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Decline and revival">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bella_-_Maori_woman_with_husband_and_child_and_2_dogs_in_front_of_home_at_Hot_Springs_LCCN2004707870.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="392" data-file-width="560" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Bella_-_Maori_woman_with_husband_and_child_and_2_dogs_in_front_of_home_at_Hot_Springs_LCCN2004707870.jpg/200px-Bella_-_Maori_woman_with_husband_and_child_and_2_dogs_in_front_of_home_at_Hot_Springs_LCCN2004707870.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Bella_-_Maori_woman_with_husband_and_child_and_2_dogs_in_front_of_home_at_Hot_Springs_LCCN2004707870.jpg/300px-Bella_-_Maori_woman_with_husband_and_child_and_2_dogs_in_front_of_home_at_Hot_Springs_LCCN2004707870.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Bella_-_Maori_woman_with_husband_and_child_and_2_dogs_in_front_of_home_at_Hot_Springs_LCCN2004707870.jpg/400px-Bella_-_Maori_woman_with_husband_and_child_and_2_dogs_in_front_of_home_at_Hot_Springs_LCCN2004707870.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Bella, a M&#257;ori woman with her husband, child and two dogs in front of their home at <a href="/wiki/Whakarewarewa" title="Whakarewarewa">Whakarewarewa</a>, 1895</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maori_woman_and_children_playing_cards_on_doorstep_of_home_-_Whakarewarewa_LCCN2004707876.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="420" data-file-width="514" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Maori_woman_and_children_playing_cards_on_doorstep_of_home_-_Whakarewarewa_LCCN2004707876.jpg/200px-Maori_woman_and_children_playing_cards_on_doorstep_of_home_-_Whakarewarewa_LCCN2004707876.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Maori_woman_and_children_playing_cards_on_doorstep_of_home_-_Whakarewarewa_LCCN2004707876.jpg/300px-Maori_woman_and_children_playing_cards_on_doorstep_of_home_-_Whakarewarewa_LCCN2004707876.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Maori_woman_and_children_playing_cards_on_doorstep_of_home_-_Whakarewarewa_LCCN2004707876.jpg/400px-Maori_woman_and_children_playing_cards_on_doorstep_of_home_-_Whakarewarewa_LCCN2004707876.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">M&#257;ori woman and children playing cards on doorstep of their home, <a href="/wiki/Whakarewarewa" title="Whakarewarewa">Whakarewarewa</a>, 1895</div></div></div></div>\n<p>By the late 19th century a widespread belief existed amongst both P&#257;keh&#257; and M&#257;ori that the M&#257;ori population would cease to exist as a separate race or culture, and become assimilated into the European population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> In 1840, New Zealand had a M&#257;ori population of about 50,000 to 70,000 and only about 2,000 Europeans. By 1860 the Europeans had increased to 50,000. The M&#257;ori population had declined to 37,520 in the 1871 census, although <a href="/wiki/Te_Rangi_H%C4%ABroa" title="Te Rangi H&#299;roa">Te Rangi H&#299;roa</a> (Sir Peter Buck) believed this figure was too low.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> The figure was 42,113 in the 1896 census, by which time Europeans numbered more than 700,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Professor Ian Pool noticed that as late as 1890, 40 per cent of all female M&#257;ori children who were born died before the age of one, a much higher rate than for males.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The decline of the M&#257;ori population did not continue; it stabilized and began to recover. By 1936 the M&#257;ori figure was 82,326, although the sudden rise in the 1930s was probably due to the introduction of the family benefit &#8722; payable only when a birth was registered, according to Professor Pool. Despite a substantial level of <a href="/wiki/Interracial_marriage" title="Interracial marriage">intermarriage</a> between the M&#257;ori and European populations, many ethnic M&#257;ori retained their cultural identity. A number of discourses developed as to the meaning of "M&#257;ori" and to who counted as M&#257;ori or not.\n</p><p>The parliament instituted four <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_electorates" title="M&#257;ori electorates">M&#257;ori seats</a> in 1867, giving all M&#257;ori men universal suffrage,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ElectionsNZ_Maori_vote_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-ElectionsNZ_Maori_vote-78">[78]</a></sup> 12 years ahead of their European New Zealand counterparts. Until the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Zealand_general_election,_1879" title="New Zealand general election, 1879">1879 general elections</a>, men had to satisfy property requirements of landowning or rental payments to qualify as voters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> New Zealand was thus the first neo-European nation in the world to give the vote to its indigenous people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> While the M&#257;ori seats encouraged M&#257;ori participation in politics, the relative size of the M&#257;ori population of the time <i>vis &#224; vis</i> <a href="/wiki/P%C4%81keh%C4%81" title="P&#257;keh&#257;">P&#257;keh&#257;</a> would have warranted approximately 15 seats. \n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ApiranaNgata05.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="536" data-file-width="402" decoding="async" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/ApiranaNgata05.jpg/200px-ApiranaNgata05.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/ApiranaNgata05.jpg/300px-ApiranaNgata05.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/ApiranaNgata05.jpg/400px-ApiranaNgata05.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ApiranaNgata05.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sir <a href="/wiki/%C4%80pirana_Ngata" title="&#256;pirana Ngata">&#256;pirana Ngata</a> became instrumental in the revival of traditional arts such as <i><a href="/wiki/Kapa_haka" title="Kapa haka">kapa haka</a></i> and carving.</div></div></div>\n<p>From the late 19th century, successful M&#257;ori politicians such as <a href="/wiki/James_Carroll_(New_Zealand_politician)" title="James Carroll (New Zealand politician)">James Carroll</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C4%80pirana_Ngata" title="&#256;pirana Ngata">&#256;pirana Ngata</a>, <a href="/wiki/Te_Rangi_H%C4%ABroa" title="Te Rangi H&#299;roa">Te Rangi H&#299;roa</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maui_Pomare" title="Maui Pomare">Maui Pomare</a>, were influential in politics. At one point Carroll became Acting <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_New_Zealand" title="Prime Minister of New Zealand">Prime Minister</a>. The group, known as the <a href="/wiki/Young_M%C4%81ori_Party" title="Young M&#257;ori Party">Young M&#257;ori Party</a>, cut across voting-blocs in Parliament and aimed to revitalise the M&#257;ori people after the devastation of the previous century. They believed the future path called for a degree of <a href="/wiki/Cultural_assimilation" title="Cultural assimilation">assimilation</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> with M&#257;ori adopting European practices such as <a href="/wiki/History_of_medicine#Modern_medicine" title="History of medicine">Western medicine</a> and education, especially learning English.\n</p><p>Ngata acted as a major force behind the revival of arts such as <a href="/wiki/Kapa_haka" title="Kapa haka">kapa haka</a> and carving. He also enacted a programme of land development, which helped many iwi retain and develop their land. Ngata became very close to <a href="/wiki/Te_Puea_Herangi" title="Te Puea Herangi">Te Puea</a>, the Waikato kingite leader, who was supported by the government in her attempt to improve living conditions for Waikato. Ngata transferred four blocks of land to Te Puea and her husband and arranged extensive government grants and loans. Ngata sacked the pakeha farm development officer and replaced him with Te Puea. He arranged for her to have a car to travel around the various farms. Te Puea\'s husband was also given a large farm at Tikitere near Rotorua. The public, media and parliament became alarmed at the flow of funds from government to Te Puea during the recession. A Royal Commission was held in 1934 that found Ngata guilty of maladministration and misappropriation of funds to the value of 500,000 pounds. Ngata was forced to resign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_World_War" title="First World War">First World War</a>, a M&#257;ori pioneer force was taken to Egypt but quickly was turned into a successful combat infantry battalion; in the last years of the war it was known as the <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_Battalion" title="M&#257;ori Battalion">M&#257;ori Battalion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> It mainly comprised Te Arawa, Te Aitanga-a-M&#257;haki, Te Aitanga-a-Hauiti, Ng&#257;ti Porou and Ng&#257;ti Kahungun and later many Cook Islanders; the Waikato and Taranaki tribes refused to enlist or be <a href="/wiki/Conscription" title="Conscription">conscripted</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>\n</p><p>M&#257;ori were badly hit by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1918_influenza_epidemic" title="1918 influenza epidemic">1918 influenza epidemic</a> when the M&#257;ori battalion returned from the Western Front. The death rate from influenza for M&#257;ori was 4.5 times higher than for Pakeha. Many M&#257;ori, especially in the Waikato, were very reluctant to visit a doctor and went to a hospital only when the patient was nearly dead. To cope with isolation, Waikato M&#257;ori, under Te Puea\'s leadership, increasingly returned to the old <a href="/wiki/Pai_M%C4%81rire" title="Pai M&#257;rire">Pai M&#257;rire</a> (Hau hau) cult of the 1860s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Until 1893, 53 years after the Treaty of Waitangi, M&#257;ori did not pay tax on land holdings. In 1893 a very light tax was payable only on leasehold land, and it was not till 1917 that M&#257;ori were required to pay a heavier tax equal to half that paid by other New Zealanders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_World_War" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>, the government decided to exempt M&#257;ori from the conscription that applied to other citizens. The M&#257;ori volunteered in large numbers, forming the 28th or <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_Battalion" title="M&#257;ori Battalion">M&#257;ori Battalion</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Response_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Response-87">[87]</a></sup> which performed creditably, notably in <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a> and Italy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Altogether 16,000 M&#257;ori took part in the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WW2_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-WW2-89">[89]</a></sup> M&#257;ori, including Cook Islanders, made up 12 per cent of the total New Zealand force. 3,600 served in the M&#257;ori Battalion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Response_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-Response-87">[87]</a></sup> the remainder serving in artillery, pioneers, home guard, infantry, airforce, and navy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WW2_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-WW2-89">[89]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Whina_Cooper_in_Hamilton.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="567" data-file-width="427" decoding="async" height="239" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Whina_Cooper_in_Hamilton.jpg/180px-Whina_Cooper_in_Hamilton.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Whina_Cooper_in_Hamilton.jpg/270px-Whina_Cooper_in_Hamilton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Whina_Cooper_in_Hamilton.jpg/360px-Whina_Cooper_in_Hamilton.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Whina_Cooper_in_Hamilton.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Whina_Cooper" title="Whina Cooper">Whina Cooper</a> leads the M&#257;ori Land March through Hamilton in 1975, seeking redress for historical grievances.</div></div></div>\n<h3><span id="Recent_history_.281960s.E2.80.93present.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_history_(1960s&#8211;present)">Recent history (1960s&#8211;present)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Recent history (1960s&#8211;present)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Since the 1960s, M&#257;oridom has undergone a cultural revival<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> concurrent with activism for social justice and a <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_protest_movement" title="M&#257;ori protest movement">protest movement</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>\nGovernment recognition of the growing political power of M&#257;ori and political activism have led to limited <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Waitangi_claims_and_settlements" title="Treaty of Waitangi claims and settlements">redress</a> for confiscation of land and for the violation of other <a href="/wiki/Property" title="Property">property rights</a>. In 1975 the Crown set up the <a href="/wiki/Waitangi_Tribunal" title="Waitangi Tribunal">Waitangi Tribunal</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> a body with the powers of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commission_of_Enquiry" title="Commission of Enquiry">Commission of Enquiry</a>, to investigate and make recommendations on such issues, but it cannot make binding rulings; the Government need not accept the findings of the Waitangi Tribunal, and has rejected some of them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Since 1976, people of M&#257;ori descent may choose to enroll on either the general or M&#257;ori roll, and vote in either the M&#257;ori only or general electorates, but not both.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seats_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-seats-94">[94]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the 1990s and 2000s, the government negotiated with M&#257;ori to provide redress for breaches by the Crown of the guarantees set out in the Treaty of Waitangi in 1840. By 2006 the government had provided over NZ$900 million in settlements, much of it in the form of land deals. The largest settlement, signed on 25 June 2008 with seven M&#257;ori iwi, transferred nine large tracts of forested land to M&#257;ori control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> As a result of the redress paid to many iwi, M&#257;ori now have significant interests in the fishing and forestry industries. There is a growing M&#257;ori leadership who are using the treaty settlements as an investment platform for economic development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HeraldJames_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-HeraldJames-96">[96]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Despite a growing acceptance of M&#257;ori culture in wider New Zealand society, the settlements have generated controversy on both sides. Some M&#257;ori have complained that the settlements occur at a level of between 1 and 2.5 cents on the dollar of the value of the confiscated lands; conversely, some non-M&#257;ori denounce the settlements and socioeconomic iniatives as amounting to race-based preferential treatment. Both of these sentiments were expressed during the <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_foreshore_and_seabed_controversy" title="New Zealand foreshore and seabed controversy">New Zealand foreshore and seabed controversy</a> in 2004.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In the <a href="/wiki/2013_New_Zealand_census" title="2013 New Zealand census">2013 census</a>, 598,605 people identified as being part of the M&#257;ori ethnic group, accounting for 14.9 per cent of the New Zealand population, while 668,724 people (17.5 per cent) claimed M&#257;ori descent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quickstats_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Quickstats-97">[97]</a></sup> Of those identifying as M&#257;ori, 278,199 people identified as of sole M&#257;ori ethnicity while 260,229 identified as of both European and M&#257;ori ethnicity, due to the high rate of intermarriage between the two cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> Under the M&#257;ori Affairs Amendment Act 1974, a M&#257;ori is defined as "a person of the M&#257;ori race of New Zealand; and includes any descendant of such a person".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to the 2013 census, the largest iwi by population is <a href="/wiki/Ng%C4%81puhi" title="Ng&#257;puhi">Ng&#257;puhi</a> (125,601), followed by <a href="/wiki/Ng%C4%81ti_Porou" title="Ng&#257;ti Porou">Ng&#257;ti Porou</a> (71,049), <a href="/wiki/Ng%C4%81i_Tahu" title="Ng&#257;i Tahu">Ng&#257;i Tahu</a> (54,819) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waikato_(iwi)" title="Waikato (iwi)">Waikato</a> (40,083). However, over 110,000 people of M&#257;ori descent could not identify their iwi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quickstats_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-Quickstats-97">[97]</a></sup> Outside of New
