Zealand, a large M&#257;ori population exists in Australia, estimated at 155,000 in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hamer_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hamer-100">[100]</a></sup> The M&#257;ori Party has suggested a special seat should be created in the New Zealand parliament representing M&#257;ori in Australia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Smaller communities also exist in the United Kingdom (approx. 8,000), the United States (up to 3,500) and Canada (approx. 1,000).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walrond_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-Walrond-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uscb_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-uscb-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-statcan_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-statcan-5">[5]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_culture" title="M&#257;ori culture">M&#257;ori culture</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KupeWheke.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1570" data-file-width="389" decoding="async" height="484" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/KupeWheke.jpg/120px-KupeWheke.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/KupeWheke.jpg/180px-KupeWheke.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/KupeWheke.jpg/240px-KupeWheke.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KupeWheke.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Late 20th-century <a href="/wiki/Whakairo" title="Whakairo">carving</a> depicting the mythological navigator <a href="/wiki/Kupe" title="Kupe">Kupe</a> fighting two sea creatures.</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditional_culture">Traditional culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Traditional culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MaoriChief1784.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2635" data-file-width="1995" decoding="async" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/MaoriChief1784.jpg/180px-MaoriChief1784.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/MaoriChief1784.jpg/270px-MaoriChief1784.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/MaoriChief1784.jpg/360px-MaoriChief1784.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MaoriChief1784.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A M&#257;ori chief with tattoos (<a href="/wiki/T%C4%81_moko" title="T&#257; moko">moko</a>) seen by James Cook and his crew.</div></div></div>\n<p>The ancestors of the M&#257;ori arrived from eastern Polynesia during the 13th century, bringing with them Polynesian cultural customs and beliefs. Early European researchers, such as <a href="/wiki/Julius_von_Haast" title="Julius von Haast">Julius von Haast</a>, a geologist, incorrectly interpreted archaeological remains as belonging to a pre-M&#257;ori <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">Paleolithic</a> people; later researchers, notably <a href="/wiki/Percy_Smith_(ethnologist)" title="Percy Smith (ethnologist)">Percy Smith</a>, magnified such theories into an elaborate scenario with a series of sharply-defined cultural stages which had M&#257;ori arriving in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Fleet" title="Great Fleet">Great Fleet</a> in 1350 and replacing the so-called "moa-hunter" culture with a "classical M&#257;ori" culture based on horticulture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> The development of M&#257;ori <a href="/wiki/Material_culture" title="Material culture">material culture</a> has been similarly delineated by the <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_New_Zealand_Te_Papa_Tongarewa" title="Museum of New Zealand Te Papa Tongarewa">Museum of New Zealand Te Papa Tongarewa</a> into "cultural periods", from the earlier "Ng&#257; Kakano" stage to the later "Te Tipunga" period, before the "Classic" period of M&#257;ori history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NgaKakano_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-NgaKakano-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="ref_Noteiv"><a href="#endnote_Noteiv">[iv]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tipunga_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-tipunga-104">[104]</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, the archaeological record indicates a gradual evolution of a <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">neolithic</a> culture that varied in pace and extent according to local resources and conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> In the course of a few centuries, the growing population led to competition for resources and an increase in warfare. The archaeological record reveals an increased frequency of fortified <a href="/wiki/P%C4%81" title="P&#257;">p&#257;</a>, although debate continues about the amount of conflict. Various systems arose which aimed to conserve resources; most of these, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Tapu_(Polynesian_culture)" title="Tapu (Polynesian culture)">tapu</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rahui" title="Rahui">r&#257;hui</a></i>, used religious or supernatural threats to discourage people from taking species at particular seasons or from specified areas.\n</p><p>Warfare between tribes was common, generally over land conflicts or to restore <i lang="mi" title="M&#257;ori language text"><a href="/wiki/Mana" title="Mana">mana</a></i>. Fighting was carried out between <i lang="mi" title="M&#257;ori language text">hap&#363;</i>. Although not practised during times of peace, M&#257;ori would sometimes eat their conquered enemies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> As M&#257;ori continued in geographic isolation, performing arts such as the <a href="/wiki/Haka" title="Haka">haka</a> developed from their Polynesian roots, as did carving and weaving. Regional dialects arose, with differences in vocabulary and in the pronunciation of some words. In 1819 two young northern chiefs, Tuai and Titere,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> who had learnt to speak and write English, went to London, where they met the language expert Samuel Lee. They stayed with a school teacher, Hall, who they told that even in Northern New Zealand there were "different languages and dialects".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> The language retained enough similarities to other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Polynesian_languages" title="Eastern Polynesian languages">Eastern Polynesian languages</a>, to the point where <a href="/wiki/Tupaia_(navigator)" title="Tupaia (navigator)">Tupaia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Tahitians" title="Tahitians">Tahitian</a> navigator on <a href="/wiki/James_Cook" title="James Cook">James Cook</a>\'s first voyage in the region acted as an interpreter between M&#257;ori and the crew of the <i><a href="/wiki/HMS_Endeavour" title="HMS Endeavour">Endeavour</a></i>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Belief_and_religion">Belief and religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Belief and religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_mythology" title="M&#257;ori mythology">M&#257;ori mythology</a> and <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_religion" title="M&#257;ori religion">M&#257;ori religion</a></div>\n<p>Traditional M&#257;ori beliefs have their origins in Polynesian culture. Many stories from M&#257;ori mythology are analogous with stories across the Pacific Ocean. Polynesian concepts such as <i><a href="/wiki/Tapu_(Polynesian_culture)" title="Tapu (Polynesian culture)">tapu</a></i> (sacred), <i><a href="/wiki/Tapu_(Polynesian_culture)#Noa" title="Tapu (Polynesian culture)">noa</a></i> (non-sacred), <i><a href="/wiki/Mana" title="Mana">mana</a></i> (authority/prestige) and <i>wairua</i> (spirit) governed everyday M&#257;ori living. These practices remained until the arrival of Europeans, when much of M&#257;ori religion and mythology was supplanted by Christianity. Today, M&#257;ori "tend to be followers of <a href="/wiki/Presbyterianism" title="Presbyterianism">Presbyterianism</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Church_of_Jesus_Christ_of_Latter-day_Saints" title="The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints">The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints</a> (Mormons), or M&#257;ori Christian groups such as <a href="/wiki/R%C4%81tana" title="R&#257;tana">R&#257;tana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ringat%C5%AB" title="Ringat&#363;">Ringat&#363;</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-religion_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-religion-109">[109]</a></sup> but with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholic" title="Catholic">Catholic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglican" title="Anglican">Anglican</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methodist" title="Methodist">Methodist</a> groupings also prominent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_New_Zealand" title="Islam in New Zealand">Islam</a> is estimated as the fastest growing religion among M&#257;ori,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> yet <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_Muslims" title="M&#257;ori Muslims">M&#257;ori Muslims</a> constitute a very small proportion of M&#257;ori.\n</p><p>At the <a href="/wiki/2013_New_Zealand_census" title="2013 New Zealand census">2013 New Zealand census</a>, 8.8 percent of M&#257;ori were affiliated with M&#257;ori Christian denominations and 39.6 percent with other Christian denominations; 46.3 percent of M&#257;ori claimed <a href="/wiki/Irreligion_in_New_Zealand" title="Irreligion in New Zealand">no religion</a>. Proportions of Christian and irreligious M&#257;ori are comparable with European New Zealanders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Young_Maori_man_dancing.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3024" data-file-width="2006" decoding="async" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Young_Maori_man_dancing.jpg/180px-Young_Maori_man_dancing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Young_Maori_man_dancing.jpg/270px-Young_Maori_man_dancing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Young_Maori_man_dancing.jpg/360px-Young_Maori_man_dancing.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Young_Maori_man_dancing.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A young man performs in a <a href="/wiki/Kapa_haka" title="Kapa haka">kapa haka</a> group at a <a href="/wiki/Rotorua" title="Rotorua">Rotorua</a> tourist venue.</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Performing_arts">Performing arts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Performing arts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><i><a href="/wiki/Kapa_haka" title="Kapa haka">Kapa haka</a></i> (literally "haka team") is a traditional M&#257;ori performance art, encompassing many forms, that is still popular today. It includes <i><a href="/wiki/Haka" title="Haka">haka</a></i> (posture dance), <a href="/wiki/Poi_(performance_art)" title="Poi (performance art)"><i>poi</i></a> (dance accompanied by song and rhythmic movements of the poi, a light ball on a string), <i>waiata-&#257;-ringa</i> (action songs) and <i>waiata koroua</i> (traditional chants). From the early 20th century kapa haka concert parties began touring overseas.\n</p><p>Since 1972 there has been a regular competition, the <a href="/wiki/Te_Matatini" title="Te Matatini">Te Matatini</a> National Festival, organised by the Aotearoa Traditional M&#257;ori Performing Arts Society. M&#257;ori from different regions send representative groups to compete in the biennial competition. There are also kapa haka groups in schools, tertiary institutions and workplaces. It is also performed at tourist venues across the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Diamond_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Diamond-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swarbrick_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Swarbrick-115">[115]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature_and_media">Literature and media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Literature and media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_art#Prehistoric_art" title="New Zealand art">New Zealand art &#167;&#160;Prehistoric art</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_art#Traditional_M&#257;ori_art" title="New Zealand art">New Zealand art &#167;&#160;Traditional M&#257;ori art</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dendroglyph" title="Dendroglyph">Dendroglyph</a></div>\n<p>Like other cultures, oral folklore was used by M&#257;ori to preserve their stories and beliefs across many centuries. In the 19th century, European-style literacy was brought to the M&#257;ori, which led to M&#257;ori history documentation in books, novels and later television. M&#257;ori language use began to decline in the 20th century with English as the language through which M&#257;ori literature became widespread.\n</p><p>Notable M&#257;ori novelists include <a href="/wiki/Patricia_Grace" title="Patricia Grace">Patricia Grace</a>, <a href="/wiki/Witi_Ihimaera" title="Witi Ihimaera">Witi Ihimaera</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alan_Duff" title="Alan Duff">Alan Duff</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Once_Were_Warriors_(film)" title="Once Were Warriors (film)">Once Were Warriors</a></i>, a 1994 film adapted from a 1990 <a href="/wiki/Once_Were_Warriors" title="Once Were Warriors">novel of the same name</a> by Alan Duff, brought the plight of some urban M&#257;ori to a wide audience. It was the highest-grossing film in New Zealand until 2006,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> and received international acclaim, winning several international film prizes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> While some M&#257;ori feared that viewers would consider the violent male characters an accurate portrayal of M&#257;ori men, most critics praised it as exposing the raw side of <a href="/wiki/Domestic_violence" title="Domestic violence">domestic violence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> Some M&#257;ori opinion, particularly feminist, welcomed the debate on domestic violence that the film enabled.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>Well-known M&#257;ori actors and actresses include <a href="/wiki/Temuera_Morrison" title="Temuera Morrison">Temuera Morrison</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cliff_Curtis" title="Cliff Curtis">Cliff Curtis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Makoare" title="Lawrence Makoare">Lawrence Makoare</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manu_Bennett" title="Manu Bennett">Manu Bennett</a> and <a href="/wiki/Keisha_Castle-Hughes" title="Keisha Castle-Hughes">Keisha Castle-Hughes</a>. They are in films like <i><a href="/wiki/Whale_Rider" title="Whale Rider">Whale Rider</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Wars:_Episode_III_%E2%80%93_Revenge_of_the_Sith" title="Star Wars: Episode III &#8211; Revenge of the Sith">Star Wars: Episode III &#8211; Revenge of the Sith</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Matrix" title="The Matrix">The Matrix</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/King_Kong" title="King Kong">King Kong</a></i>, <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=The_River_Queen&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="The River Queen (page does not exist)">The River Queen</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lord_of_the_Rings_(film_series)" title="The Lord of the Rings (film series)">The Lord of The Rings</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rapa_Nui_(film)" title="Rapa Nui (film)">Rapa Nui</a></i>, and others, and famous television series like <i><a href="/wiki/Xena:_Warrior_Princess" title="Xena: Warrior Princess">Xena: Warrior Princess</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Hercules:_The_Legendary_Journeys" title="Hercules: The Legendary Journeys">Hercules: The Legendary Journeys</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Arthur_Conan_Doyle%27s_The_Lost_World" title="Sir Arthur Conan Doyle\'s The Lost World">The Lost World</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Spartacus:_Blood_and_Sand" title="Spartacus: Blood and Sand">Spartacus: Blood and Sand</a></i>. In most cases their roles in <a href="/wiki/Hollywood" title="Hollywood">Hollywood</a> productions have them portraying ethnic groups other than M&#257;ori.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sport">Sport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Sport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Haka_2006.jpg"><img alt="Rugby team wearing all black, facing the camera, knees bent, and facing toward a team wearing white" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Haka_2006.jpg/220px-Haka_2006.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Haka_2006.jpg/330px-Haka_2006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Haka_2006.jpg/440px-Haka_2006.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Haka_2006.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Haka_(sports)" title="Haka (sports)">haka</a> performed by the <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_national_rugby_union_team" title="New Zealand national rugby union team">national rugby union team</a> before a game.</div></div></div>\n<p>M&#257;ori participate fully in <a href="/wiki/Sport_in_New_Zealand" title="Sport in New Zealand">New Zealand\'s sporting culture</a>, and are well-represented in rugby union, rugby league and netball teams at all levels. The <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_national_rugby_union_team" title="New Zealand national rugby union team">New Zealand national rugby union team</a> performs a <a href="/wiki/Haka_(sports)" title="Haka (sports)">haka</a>, a traditional M&#257;ori challenge, before international matches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> As well as participation in national sports teams, there are M&#257;ori <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_All_Blacks" title="M&#257;ori All Blacks">rugby union</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_M%C4%81ori_rugby_league_team" title="New Zealand M&#257;ori rugby league team">rugby league</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_M%C4%81ori_cricket_team" title="New Zealand M&#257;ori cricket team">cricket</a> representative teams that play in international competitions.\n</p><p>At the <a href="/wiki/2016_Summer_Olympics" title="2016 Summer Olympics">2016 Summer Olympics</a> in Rio de Janeiro, 41 of the 199 competitors (20.5 percent) were of M&#257;ori descent in the <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_at_the_2016_Summer_Olympics" title="New Zealand at the 2016 Summer Olympics">New Zealand delegation</a>, with the rugby sevens squads alone having 17 M&#257;ori competitors (out of 24). There were also three competitors of M&#257;ori descent in the <a href="/wiki/Australia_at_the_2016_Summer_Olympics" title="Australia at the 2016 Summer Olympics">Australian delegation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ki-o-rahi" title="Ki-o-rahi">Ki-o-rahi</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tapawai" title="Tapawai">tapawai</a> are two sports of M&#257;ori origin. Ki-o-rahi got an unexpected boost when <a href="/wiki/McDonald%27s" title="McDonald\'s">McDonald\'s</a> chose it to represent New Zealand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Outrigger_canoeing" title="Outrigger canoeing">Waka ama</a> (outrigger canoeing) is also popular with M&#257;ori.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_language" title="M&#257;ori language">M&#257;ori language</a> and <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_language_revival" title="M&#257;ori language revival">M&#257;ori language revival</a></div>\n<p>From about 1890, M&#257;ori <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Members_of_Parliament" title="Members of Parliament">Members of Parliament</a> realised the importance of English literacy to M&#257;ori and insisted that all M&#257;ori children be taught in English. Missionaries, who still ran many M&#257;ori schools, had been teaching exclusively in M&#257;ori but the M&#257;ori MPs insisted this should stop. However attendance at school for many M&#257;ori was intermittent. \n</p><p>The M&#257;ori language, also known as <i>te reo M&#257;ori</i> (pronounced [&#712;ma&#720;o&#638;i, te &#712;&#638;eo &#712;ma&#720;o&#638;i]) or simply <i>Te Reo</i> ("the language"), has the status of an official language. Linguists classify it within the Eastern Polynesian languages as being closely related to <a href="/wiki/Cook_Islands_M%C4%81ori" title="Cook Islands M&#257;ori">Cook Islands M&#257;ori</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tuamotuan_language" title="Tuamotuan language">Tuamotuan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tahitian_language" title="Tahitian language">Tahitian</a>. Before European contact M&#257;ori did not have a written language and "important information such as whakapapa was memorised and passed down verbally through the generations".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> M&#257;ori were familiar with the concept of maps and when interacting with missionaries in 1815 could draw accurate maps of their <a href="/wiki/Rohe" title="Rohe">rohe</a> (iwi boundaries), onto paper, that were the equal of European maps. Missionaries surmised that M&#257;ori had traditionally drawn maps on sand or other natural materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In many areas of New Zealand, M&#257;ori lost its role as a living community language used by significant numbers of people in the <a href="/wiki/Post-war" title="Post-war">post-war</a> years. In tandem with calls for sovereignty and for the righting of social injustices from the 1970s onwards, New Zealand schools now teach M&#257;ori culture and language as an option, and pre-school <i>kohanga reo</i> ("language-nests") have started, which teach <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tamariki" title="Tamariki">tamariki</a></i> (young children) exclusively in M&#257;ori. These now<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> extend right through secondary schools (<i>kura tuarua</i>). Most preschool centres teach basics such as colours, numerals and greetings in M&#257;ori songs and chants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_Television" title="M&#257;ori Television">M&#257;ori Television</a>, a government-funded channel committed to broadcasting primarily in Te Reo, began in March 2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> The 1996 census reported 160,000 M&#257;ori speakers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-quickstats_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-quickstats-127">[127]</a></sup> At the time of the 2013 census 125,352 M&#257;ori (21.3 per cent) reported a conversational level of <a href="/wiki/Language_proficiency" title="Language proficiency">proficiency</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society">Society</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Society">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_development">Historical development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Historical development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Polynesian settlers in New Zealand developed a distinct society over several hundred years. Social groups were tribal, with no unified society or single M&#257;ori identity until after the arrival of Europeans. Nevertheless, common elements could be found in all M&#257;ori groups in pre-European New Zealand, including a shared Polynesian heritage, a common basic language, familial associations, traditions of warfare, and similar mythologies and religious beliefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Most M&#257;ori lived in villages, which were inhabited by several <i><a href="/wiki/Wh%C4%81nau" title="Wh&#257;nau">wh&#257;nau</a></i> (extended families) who collectively formed a <i><a href="/wiki/Hap%C5%AB" title="Hap&#363;">hap&#363;</a></i> (clan or subtribe). Members of a hap&#363; cooperated with food production, gathering resources, raising families and defence. M&#257;ori society across New Zealand was broadly stratified into three classes of people: <i><a href="/wiki/Rangatira" title="Rangatira">rangatira</a></i>, chiefs and ruling families; <i>t&#363;t&#363;&#257;</i>, commoners; and <i>m&#333;kai</i>, slaves. <i><a href="/wiki/Tohunga" title="Tohunga">Tohunga</a></i> also held special standing in their communities as specialists of revered arts, skills and esoteric knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King-p42p43_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-King-p42p43-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TeAra-Taonui_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-TeAra-Taonui-131">[131]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Shared ancestry, intermarriage and trade strengthened relationships between different groups. Many hap&#363; with mutually-recognised shared ancestry formed <i><a href="/wiki/Iwi" title="Iwi">iwi</a></i>, or tribes, which were the largest social unit in M&#257;ori society. Hap&#363; and iwi often united for expeditions to gather food and resources, or in times of conflict. In contrast, warfare developed as an integral part of traditional life, as different groups competed for food and resources, settled personal disputes, and sought to increase their prestige and authority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King-p42p43_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-King-p42p43-130">[130]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Famille_Maori_1998-1361-139.jpg"><img alt="M&#257;ori wh&#257;nau from Rotorua in the 1880s" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="657" decoding="async" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Famille_Maori_1998-1361-139.jpg/250px-Famille_Maori_1998-1361-139.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Famille_Maori_1998-1361-139.jpg/375px-Famille_Maori_1998-1361-139.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Famille_Maori_1998-1361-139.jpg/500px-Famille_Maori_1998-1361-139.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Famille_Maori_1998-1361-139.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>M&#257;ori wh&#257;nau from <a href="/wiki/Rotorua" title="Rotorua">Rotorua</a> in the 1880s. Many aspects of Western life and culture, including European clothing and architecture, became incorporated into M&#257;ori society during the 19th century.</div></div></div>\n<p>The arrival of Europeans to New Zealand dates back to the 17th century, although it was not until the expeditions of James Cook over a hundred years later that any meaningful interactions occurred between Europeans and M&#257;ori. For M&#257;ori, the new arrivals brought opportunities for trade, which many groups embraced eagerly. Early European settlers introduced tools, weapons, clothing and foods to M&#257;ori across New Zealand, in exchange for resources, land and labour. M&#257;ori began selectively adopting elements of Western society during the 19th century, including European clothing and food, and later Western education, religion and architecture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup>\n</p><p>But as the 19th century wore on, relations between European colonial settlers and different M&#257;ori groups became increasingly strained. Tensions led to conflict in the 1860s, and the <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_land_confiscations" title="New Zealand land confiscations">confiscation of millions of acres of M&#257;ori land</a>. Significant amounts of land were also purchased by the colonial government and later through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Native_Land_Court" title="Native Land Court">Native Land Court</a>.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>By the start of the 20th century, a greater awareness had emerged of a unified M&#257;ori identity, particularly in comparison to P&#257;keh&#257;, who now overwhelmingly outnumbered the M&#257;ori as a whole. M&#257;ori and P&#257;keh&#257; societies remained largely separate&#8212;socially, culturally, economically and geographically&#8212;for much of the 19th and early 20th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> The key reason for this was that M&#257;ori remained almost exclusively a rural population, whereas increasingly the European population was urban especially after 1900. Nevertheless, M&#257;ori groups continued to engage with the government and in legal processes to increase their standing in (and ultimately further their incorporation into) wider New Zealand society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> The main point of contact with the government were the four M&#257;ori Members of Parliament.\n</p><p>Many M&#257;ori <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urbanisation" title="Urbanisation">migrated to larger rural towns and cities</a> during the Depression and post-WWII periods in search of employment, leaving rural communities depleted and disconnecting many urban M&#257;ori from their traditional social controls and tribal homelands. Yet while standards of living improved among M&#257;ori, they continued to lag behind P&#257;keh&#257; in areas such as health, income, skilled employment and access to higher levels of education. M&#257;ori leaders and government policymakers alike struggled to deal with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_issues" title="Social issues">social issues</a> stemming from increased urban migration, including a shortage of housing and jobs, and a rise in urban crime, poverty and health problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sorrenson_1997,_pp_339&#8211;41_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sorrenson_1997,_pp_339&#8211;41-135">[135]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In regards to housing, a 1961 census revealed significant differences in the living conditions of M&#257;ori and Europeans. That year, out of all the (unshared) non-M&#257;ori private dwellings in New Zealand, 96.8 per cent had a bath or shower, 94.1 per cent a hot water service, 88.7 per cent a flush toilet, 81.6 per cent a refrigerator, and 78.6 per cent an electric washing machine. By contrast, for all (unshared) M&#257;ori private dwellings that same year, 76.8 per cent had a bath or shower, 68.9 per cent a hot water service, 55.8 per cent a refrigerator, 54.1 per cent a flush toilet, and 47 per cent an electric washing machine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup>\n</p><p>While the arrival of Europeans had a profound impact on the M&#257;ori way of life, many aspects of traditional society have survived into the 21st century. M&#257;ori participate fully in all spheres of New Zealand culture and society, leading largely Western lifestyles while also maintaining their own cultural and social customs. The traditional social strata of <i>rangatira</i>, <i>t&#363;t&#363;&#257;</i> and <i>m&#333;kai</i> have all but disappeared from M&#257;ori society, while the roles of tohunga and <a href="/wiki/Kaum%C4%81tua" title="Kaum&#257;tua">kaum&#257;tua</a> are still present. Traditional kinship ties are also actively maintained, and the wh&#257;nau in particular remains an integral part of M&#257;ori life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Marae.2C_hap.C5.AB_and_iwi"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Marae,_hap&#363;_and_iwi">Marae, hap&#363; and iwi</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Marae, hap&#363; and iwi">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Whenuakura_Marae,_Taranaki,_New_Zealand.jpg"><img alt="Whenuakura Marae in Taranaki" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2138" data-file-width="2800" decoding="async" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Whenuakura_Marae%2C_Taranaki%2C_New_Zealand.jpg/250px-Whenuakura_Marae%2C_Taranaki%2C_New_Zealand.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Whenuakura_Marae%2C_Taranaki%2C_New_Zealand.jpg/375px-Whenuakura_Marae%2C_Taranaki%2C_New_Zealand.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Whenuakura_Marae%2C_Taranaki%2C_New_Zealand.jpg/500px-Whenuakura_Marae%2C_Taranaki%2C_New_Zealand.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Whenuakura_Marae,_Taranaki,_New_Zealand.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Whenuakura" title="Whenuakura">Whenuakura</a> Marae in Taranaki. Marae continue to function as local community centres in modern M&#257;ori society.</div></div></div>\n<p>M&#257;ori society at a local level is particularly visible at the <a href="/wiki/Marae" title="Marae">marae</a>. Formerly the central meeting spaces in traditional villages, marae today usually comprise a group of buildings around an open space, that frequently host events such as weddings, funerals, church services and other large gatherings, with traditional protocol and etiquette usually observed. They also serve as the base of one or sometimes several hap&#363;.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Most M&#257;ori affiliate with one or more iwi (and hap&#363;), based on genealogical descent (<i><a href="/wiki/Whakapapa" title="Whakapapa">whakapapa</a></i>). Iwi vary in size, from a few hundred members to over 100,000 in the case of Ng&#257;puhi. Many people do not live in their traditional tribal regions as a result of urban migration.\n</p><p>Iwi are usually governed by <a href="/wiki/R%C5%ABnanga" title="R&#363;nanga">r&#363;nanga</a> (governing councils or trust boards) which represent the iwi in consultations and negotiations with the New Zealand government. R&#363;nanga also manage tribal assets and spearhead health, education, economic and social initiatives to help iwi members.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Socioeconomic_challenges">Socioeconomic challenges</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Socioeconomic challenges">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>M&#257;ori on average have fewer assets than the rest of the population, and run greater risks of many negative economic and social outcomes. Over 50 per cent of M&#257;ori live in areas in the three highest deprivation deciles, compared with 24 per cent of the rest of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup>\nAlthough M&#257;ori make up only 14 per cent of the population, they make up almost 50 per cent of the prison population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup>\n</p><p>M&#257;ori have higher unemployment-rates than other cultures resident in New Zealand <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup>\nM&#257;ori have higher numbers of suicides than non-M&#257;ori.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup>\n"Only 47 per cent of M&#257;ori school-leavers finish school with qualifications higher than <a href="/wiki/National_Certificate_of_Educational_Achievement" title="National Certificate of Educational Achievement">NCEA</a> Level One; compared to 74 per cent European; 87 per cent Asian."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup>\nAlthough New Zealand rates very well globally in the PISA rankings that compare national performance in reading, science and maths, "once you disaggregate the PISA scores, Pakeha students are second in the world and M&#257;ori are 34th."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup>\nM&#257;ori suffer more health problems, including higher levels of alcohol and drug abuse, smoking and obesity. Less frequent use of healthcare services mean that late diagnosis and treatment intervention lead to higher levels of morbidity and mortality in many manageable conditions, such as <a href="/wiki/Cervical_cancer" title="Cervical cancer">cervical cancer</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diabetes" title="Diabetes">diabetes</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> per head of population than non-M&#257;ori.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> Although M&#257;ori life expectancy rates have increased dramatically in the last 50 years, they still have considerably lower life-expectancies compared to New Zealanders of European ancestry: in 2004, M&#257;ori males lived 69.0 years vs. non-M&#257;ori males 77.2 years; M&#257;ori females 73.2 yrs vs. non-M&#257;ori females 81.9 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> This gap had narrowed by 2013: 72.8 years for men and 76.5 years for women, compared to 80.2 years for non-M&#257;ori men and 83.7 years for non-M&#257;ori women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> Also, a recent study by the New Zealand Family Violence Clearinghouse showed that M&#257;ori women and children are more likely to experience domestic violence than any other ethnic group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Race_relations">Race relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Race relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Hori_(racial_slur)" title="Hori (racial slur)">Hori (racial slur)</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hikoi_011.jpg"><img alt="Protest hikoi during the Foreshore and Seabed controversy in 2004" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="799" decoding="async" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Hikoi_011.jpg/140px-Hikoi_011.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Hikoi_011.jpg/210px-Hikoi_011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Hikoi_011.jpg/280px-Hikoi_011.jpg 2x" width="140"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hikoi_011.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Protest <a href="/wiki/Hikoi" title="Hikoi">hikoi</a> during the <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_foreshore_and_seabed_controversy" title="New Zealand foreshore and seabed controversy">Foreshore and seabed controversy</a> in 2004.</div></div></div>\n<p>The status of M&#257;ori as the indigenous people of New Zealand is recognised in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Zealand_law" title="New Zealand law">New Zealand law</a> by the term <i><a href="/wiki/Tangata_whenua" title="Tangata whenua">tangata whenua</a></i> (lit.&#160;"people of the land"), which identifies the traditional connection between M&#257;ori and a given area of land. M&#257;ori as a whole can be considered as tangata whenua of New Zealand entirely; individual iwi are recognised as tangata whenua for areas of New Zealand in which they are traditionally based, while hap&#363; are tangata whenua within their marae. New Zealand law periodically requires consultation between the government and tangata whenua&#8212;for example, during major land development projects. This usually takes the form of negotiations between local or national government and the r&#363;nanga of one or more relevant iwi, although the government generally decides which (if any) concerns are acted upon.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NZ_delegation_UN_Forum_on_Indigenous_Issues.jpg"><img alt="New Zealand endorses Declaration on the Rights of Indigenous People, 2010" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/NZ_delegation_UN_Forum_on_Indigenous_Issues.jpg/230px-NZ_delegation_UN_Forum_on_Indigenous_Issues.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/NZ_delegation_UN_Forum_on_Indigenous_Issues.jpg/345px-NZ_delegation_UN_Forum_on_Indigenous_Issues.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/NZ_delegation_UN_Forum_on_Indigenous_Issues.jpg/460px-NZ_delegation_UN_Forum_on_Indigenous_Issues.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NZ_delegation_UN_Forum_on_Indigenous_Issues.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>New Zealand endorses the United Nations <a href="/wiki/Declaration_on_the_Rights_of_Indigenous_Peoples" title="Declaration on the Rights of Indigenous Peoples">Declaration on the Rights of Indigenous Peoples</a> in April 2010.</div></div></div>\n<p>M&#257;ori issues are a prominent feature of race relations in New Zealand. Historically, many P&#257;keh&#257; viewed race relations in their country as being the "best in the world", a view that prevailed until M&#257;ori urban migration in the mid-20th century brought cultural and socioeconomic differences to wider attention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup>\n</p><p>M&#257;ori protest movements grew significantly in the 1960s and 1970s seeking redress for past grievances, particularly in regard to land rights. Successive governments have responded by enacting <a href="/wiki/Affirmative_action" title="Affirmative action">affirmative action</a> programmes, funding cultural rejuvenation initiatives and negotiating tribal settlements for past breaches of the Treaty of Waitangi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> Further efforts have focused on cultural preservation and reducing socioeconomic disparity.\n</p><p>Nevertheless, race relations remains a contentious issue in New Zealand society. M&#257;ori advocates continue to push for further redress claiming that their concerns are being marginalised or ignored. A 2007 <a href="/wiki/Department_of_Corrections_(New_Zealand)" title="Department of Corrections (New Zealand)">Department of Corrections</a> report found that M&#257;ori are disproportionately represented in the criminal justice system not only because they commit more crimes but also because they face prejudice at many levels: "a number of studies have shown evidence of greater likelihood, associated only with ethnicity, for M&#257;ori offenders to have police contact, be charged, lack legal representation, not be granted bail, plead guilty, be convicted, be sentenced to non-monetary penalties, and be denied release to Home Detention".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> Conversely, critics denounce the scale of assistance given to M&#257;ori as amounting to preferential treatment for a select group of people based on race.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> Both sentiments were highlighted during the <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_foreshore_and_seabed_controversy" title="New Zealand foreshore and seabed controversy">foreshore and seabed controversy</a> in 2004, in which the New Zealand government claimed sole ownership of the New Zealand foreshore and seabed, over the objections of M&#257;ori groups who were seeking customary title.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Commerce">Commerce</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Commerce">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_Law_Commission" title="New Zealand Law Commission">New Zealand Law Commission</a> has started a project to develop a legal framework for M&#257;ori who want to manage communal resources and responsibilities. The voluntary system proposes an alternative to existing companies, incorporations, and trusts in which tribes and hap&#363; and other groupings can interact with the legal system. The foreshadowed legislation, under the proposed name of the "Waka Umanga (M&#257;ori Corporations) Act", would provide a model adaptable to suit the needs of individual iwi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> At the end of 2009, the proposed legislation was awaiting a second hearing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Wider commercial exposure has increased public awareness of the M&#257;ori culture, but has also resulted in several notable legal disputes. Between 1998 and 2006, <a href="/wiki/Ng%C4%81ti_Toa" title="Ng&#257;ti Toa">Ng&#257;ti Toa</a> attempted to trademark the haka "<a href="/wiki/Ka_Mate" title="Ka Mate">Ka Mate</a>" to prevent its use by commercial organisations without their permission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> In 2001, Danish toymaker Lego faced legal action by several M&#257;ori tribal groups (fronted by lawyer Maui Solomon) and members of the on-line discussion forum Aotearoa Cafe for trademarking M&#257;ori words used in naming the Bionicle product range (<i>see <a href="/wiki/Bionicle#M&#257;ori_language_controversy" title="Bionicle">Bionicle M&#257;ori controversy</a></i>).\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_representation">Political representation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Political representation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_politics" title="M&#257;ori politics">M&#257;ori politics</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Papawai1897.jpg"><img alt="The M&#257;ori Parliament at P&#257;p&#257;wai, Greytown in 1897" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="485" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Papawai1897.jpg/250px-Papawai1897.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Papawai1897.jpg/375px-Papawai1897.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Papawai1897.jpg/500px-Papawai1897.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Papawai1897.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The opening of the M&#257;ori Parliament at P&#257;p&#257;wai, <a href="/wiki/Greytown,_New_Zealand" title="Greytown, New Zealand">Greytown</a> in 1897, with <a href="/wiki/Richard_Seddon" title="Richard Seddon">Richard Seddon</a> in attendance.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tino_Rangatiratanga_Maori_sovereignty_movement_flag.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="1080" decoding="async" height="122" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Tino_Rangatiratanga_Maori_sovereignty_movement_flag.svg/220px-Tino_Rangatiratanga_Maori_sovereignty_movement_flag.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Tino_Rangatiratanga_Maori_sovereignty_movement_flag.svg/330px-Tino_Rangatiratanga_Maori_sovereignty_movement_flag.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Tino_Rangatiratanga_Maori_sovereignty_movement_flag.svg/440px-Tino_Rangatiratanga_Maori_sovereignty_movement_flag.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tino_Rangatiratanga_Maori_sovereignty_movement_flag.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Flag of M&#257;ori proposed in 1998<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>M&#257;ori have been involved in New Zealand politics since the <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_the_Independence_of_New_Zealand" title="Declaration of the Independence of New Zealand">Declaration of the Independence of New Zealand</a>, before the Treaty of Waitangi was signed in 1840. M&#257;ori have had <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_electorates" title="M&#257;ori electorates">reserved seats</a> in the <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_Parliament" title="New Zealand Parliament">New Zealand Parliament</a> since 1868: presently, this accounts for seven of the 122 seats in New Zealand\'s unicameral parliament. The contesting of these seats was the first opportunity for many M&#257;ori to participate in New Zealand elections, although the elected M&#257;ori representatives initially struggled to assert significant influence. M&#257;ori received universal suffrage with other New Zealand citizens in 1893.\n</p><p>Being a traditionally tribal people, no one organisation ostensibly speaks for all M&#257;ori nationwide. The <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_King_Movement" title="M&#257;ori King Movement">M&#257;ori King Movement</a> originated in the 1860s as an attempt by several iwi to unify under one leader: in modern times, it serves a largely ceremonial role. Another attempt at political unity was the Kotahitanga Movement, which established a separate M&#257;ori Parliament that held annual sessions from 1892 until its last sitting in 1902.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup>\n</p><p>There are seven designated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_seats" title="M&#257;ori seats">M&#257;ori seats</a> in the <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_Parliament" title="New Zealand Parliament">New Zealand Parliament</a> (and M&#257;ori can and do stand in and win general roll seats), and consideration of and consultation with M&#257;ori have become routine requirements for councils and government organisations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seats_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-seats-94">[94]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Debate occurs frequently as to the relevance and legitimacy of the M&#257;ori electoral roll and seats. The National Party announced in 2008 it would abolish the seats when all historic Treaty settlements have been resolved, which it aimed to complete by 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> However, after the election National reached an agreement with the M&#257;ori Party not to abolish the seats until M&#257;ori give their approval.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Several M&#257;ori political parties have formed over the years to improve the position of M&#257;ori in New Zealand society. The present <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_Party" title="M&#257;ori Party">M&#257;ori Party</a>, formed in 2004, secured 1.32 per cent of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Party_vote" title="Party vote">party vote</a> at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Zealand_general_election,_2014" title="New Zealand general election, 2014">2014 general election</a> and held two seats in the <a href="/wiki/51st_New_Zealand_Parliament" title="51st New Zealand Parliament">51st New Zealand Parliament</a>, with two MPs serving as Ministers outside <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Zealand_Cabinet" title="New Zealand Cabinet">Cabinet</a>. The party did not achieve any representatives in the <a href="/wiki/52nd_New_Zealand_Parliament" title="52nd New Zealand Parliament">52nd New Zealand Parliament</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r853264625">.mw-parser-output .refbegin{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul{list-style-type:none;margin-left:0}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul>li,.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>dl>dd{margin-left:0;padding-left:3.2em;text-indent:-3.2em;list-style:none}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-100{font-size:100%}</style><div class="refbegin" style="">\n<ul><li><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Notei"><b><a href="#ref_Notei">^i</a></b></span>&#160;: <i>M&#257;ori</i> has cognates in other <a href="/wiki/Polynesian_languages" title="Polynesian languages">Polynesian languages</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_language" title="Hawaiian language">Hawaiian</a> <i>maoli</i>, <a href="/wiki/Tahitian_language" title="Tahitian language">Tahitian</a> <i>m&#257;\'ohi</i>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cook_Islands_Maori" title="Cook Islands Maori">Cook Islands Maori</a> <i>m&#257;ori</i> which all share similar meanings.</li>\n<li><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Noteii"><b><a href="#ref_Noteii">^ii</a></b></span>&#160;: The orthographic conventions developed by the <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_Language_Commission" title="M&#257;ori Language Commission">M&#257;ori Language Commission</a> (<i>Te Taura Whiri i te Reo M&#257;ori</i>) recommend the use of the <a href="/wiki/Macron_(diacritic)" title="Macron (diacritic)">macron</a> (&#257; &#275; &#299; &#333; &#363;) to denote long vowels. Contemporary English-language usage in New Zealand tends to avoid the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglicise" title="Anglicise">anglicised</a> plural form of the word <i>M&#257;ori</i> with an "s": The M&#257;ori language generally marks plurals by changing the <a href="/wiki/Article_(grammar)" title="Article (grammar)">article</a> rather than the noun, for example: <i>te waka</i> (the canoe); <i>ng&#257; waka</i> (the canoes).</li>\n<li><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Noteiii"><b><a href="#ref_Noteiii">^iii</a></b></span>&#160;: In 2003, <a href="/wiki/Christian_Cullen" title="Christian Cullen">Christian Cullen</a> became a member of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Zealand_M%C4%81ori_rugby_union_team" title="New Zealand M&#257;ori rugby union team">M&#257;ori rugby team</a> despite having, according to his father, about 1/64 M&#257;ori ancestry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup></li>\n<li><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Noteiv"><b><a href="#ref_Noteiv">^iv</a></b></span>&#160;: Although, as noted elsewhere in this article, evidence is increasingly pointing to 1280 as the earliest date of settlement.</li></ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://archive.stats.govt.nz/browse_for_stats/population/estimates_and_projections/MaoriPopulationEstimates_HOTPAt30Jun17.aspx" rel="nofollow">"M&#257;ori Population Estimates: At 30 June 2017 &#8211;
